Hi Ellie, hope you are good.  I feel I was very invested in the Doctor. I was born a year after the first episode aired.  Jon Pertwee was 'my doctor'.  I used to watch as the whole rest of my family would jump into the car and go out somewhere for the day every Saturday.  I would stay home on my own because, for 20 short minutes, there would be a new episode of Doctor Who.  Serial form, cliff hangers, no repeats, no catch up tv, no Sky+, not even a video recorder (ask your dad what one of those was!) to enable me to watch it at any other time than 'live'.  Great days!  Of course, it would take very skilled writing to produce something that can have broad appeal to amuse a veteran fan like myself, whilst amusing their kids, and pulling in a new generation of fans.  I remember when I tuned in for the first episode of the Jodie Whittaker run ‚Äì I switched off when it became clear it was not written for the likes of me ‚Äì the lines 'I thought you were a man' with the snarky instant comeback 'I had an upgrade', making that very clear to me ‚Äì so not equality, but just another badly penned 'women are great, men are the worst' show.  I wonder how anybody with any care about the show can tolerate what happened on Saturday ‚Äì 'Space Beast' suggested that we were in for a traditional Doctor Who romp.  However, the episode should have been called 'Stick it to the man' after all the physical, verbal, and mocking comments that we saw him have to soak up.  I think there might have been a bit of an uproar had the episode finished with 2 men telling a woman that they know everything, she knows nothing and, had she still been a man, then she would have understood.  Anyway, long way of saying that I have no interest now in anywhere else that the Doctor goes, who he travels with, who writes it, who he battles, or anything ‚Äì I have clocked out.  I share the opinion of the BBC's own TV critic.  I am blessed with access to the whole back catalogue of classic episodes due to iPlayer, and have been having hours of fun with that since it went up.  I am sure the day will come when they get digitally 'corrected for the modern audience' (e.g. Davros being deep-fake swapped-out with the new Hitler style white middle aged man version, so that he can not offend anybody, etc).  What puzzles me a little is if Whoculture loves the doctor (after all it gives it plenty of support, free promotion, all round love - seemingly, getting absolutely nothing in return ‚Äì not even an invite to a preview screening of the new episodes before they go to the public) ‚Äì then how can it accept, still support and love the direction being taken?  As with new episodes of the show itself, I also take this as my cue to say goodbye to Whoculture too because it won't have anything that I need to hear from now on.

I have loved Doctor Who ho for as long as I can remember, my dad's friend had a full size Darlek and I was terrified by Davros as child, he was the best villain in a story.  In short, I loved everything about Doctor Who and I have had the privilege of meeting a number of the doctors I saw on TV in real life, my favorite being the 4th Doctor, Tom Baker. However, In recent years the quality of writing got progressively worse and became more social commentary instead of escapist fun. Gone was the light hearted quips and clever solutions, replaced by with political ideology from writers who thought they were morally superior to the audience.   When Jodie Whittaker took the mantle on in 2017 I was hopeful for a different take and I respected her as an actor.  However, after 3 episodes I couldn't watch anymore, the show did't feel like Doctor Who any more.  I couldn't watch this anymore, so I stopped watching.  When I saw that DT was returning for 3 specials I was really excited, but concerned, I really hoped we would get a great story and return to form, just light hearted Saturday evening entertainment like we had for most of my life. I tuned in, feeling excited and hopeful, however I had to stop watching it when I tried it was bloody awful, if anything this was terrible,  such a horrible way to see this national treasure of character treated and 60 years of it's legacy defecated upon.  Story was replaced by identity politics and clever dialogue was replaced by gender based lecture, "it's ashame you are not a woman"!!!!! What the actual !.  I felt like the doctor was the antagonist and his gender was the enemy. It was  like seeing a family member getting murdered in front of me. It actually made me really angry.  It was shameful to see the BBC do this to the Doctor.   I was really hoping this would have made it watchable again but I couldn't have been more wrong.   I feel they have disrespected the character, 60 years of world building, a vast part of the audience and this has killed the Doctor for me forever.   It's a heartbreaking way to see such a big part of my life destroyed in such an evil way. Russell is the real Davros, he has managed what he never did.  He killed Doctor Who.    RIP Doctor Who 1963-2017.

There were a lot of good things in this episode, and they've been covered pretty well here, but "you couldn't think of X because you're male" is the kind of sexist that would have gotten the show cancelled immediately if the gender was any other one.   This episode suffers from a phenomenon that I'm not sure has been named, but it has certainly been observable. When I was young TV and films frequently had a "wise black mentor" figure because people were trying to be inclusive of people with different coloured skin but they were afraid to make them be substantially wrong (certainyl never fail) or  even struggle with anything meaningful beyond the most obvious things like their treatment because of their skin colour. Later everyone in film and TV had to have a wise gay friend who was always kind and knew the right thing to say, and similarly never struggled unless it was because they were being mistreated or minsunderstood because of their orientation. Now we have someone in a wheelchair working for UNIT who is always in the right place, always equipped doesn't fangirl about meeting the doctor (because she's apparently better than that) and also is in no way frustrated by the Doctor not cooperating with UNIT (because she's also better than that) where this has pretty much always been the tension dynamic between the Doctor and UNIT. And we have a trans character who inherited the metacrisis and magically can deus ex machina the plot and their only life struggle is "I don't fit in because I'm different" - we also have the recent children in need special where they took away Davros' wheelchair because we can't possibly depict a differently abled person as a bad guy (as if a person in a wheel chair is somehow immune from corruption)  I absolutely think that representation is important and good, but there's something unrealistic and false about these chracters who don't need to struggle in their life because the writers are afraid to make them real people.   Prior to the revelation that Davros had been given his legs because we can't let wheelies be bad guys I had always understood that Davros had a degenerative condition caused by his attempts to improve the daleks and he loses more of himself as time progresses. I was perfectly happy to see him with legs in his early days, knowing that he had not yet lost them, or his eyes, or whatever else he loses along the way. But the fact that this was an intenional change made with the explicit reasoning tells me that we are going to have a continuing problem of ham-fisted representation attempts where writers are walking on eggshells so as not to offend someone in a minority by showing them as a real person who has real struggles  and misght actually give in to selfishness, make a mistake, or actually do  something genuinely evil.

Down for me is that there's no explanation of where his new sonic screwdriver came from. Usually, with a new regeneration, we get scenes where the Doctor either receives a new one from the Tardis, or builds one from scratch, etc. There's a bit of inclusion of the reason for it being the way it is, what the new Doctor wants from it, etc. Even just the scene of the Doctor getting the new sonic and drooling over it, oooh that's a beauty, I can't wait to use this... It's a lovely part of the transition into the new Doctor, it's so highly tied to their character etc. And we didn't get that.   In fact we don't know how long it's been since he regenerated. It can't have been very long, since the Tardis hadn't reconfigured its interior yet. So that makes us assume he regenerated, popped inside, went to earth, landed in the market near Donna. So when would he have gotten the sonic?  If more time has actually passed, if he had some other adventure first (another commenter said something about a comic showing he got the sonic from daleks before coming to earth) then why would the Tardis not have already redecorated itself?   Whether or not the new sonic's capabilities make sense, whether or not we like the new sonic -- I just miss that we didn't get an Origin Story for it like we usually do. :(

I appreciate that the sonic shields were show to create and move otherwise it would have way too convenient a feature. I‚Äôm not sure I like it was in there at all but at least it‚Äôs not op.
There was a couple things I didn't like (which I'm sure people will be raging about for entirely different reasons) when it came to how they handled the diversity.   Towards the start of the episode, they did brilliant with Rose, and Sylvia accidentally misgendering her was a very realistic moment that I, as a trans person, have experienced plenty of times. It was nice to see how accepting Donna is of her daughter and how Sylvia is clearly trying even if she slips up occasionally.  However, there were a few moments that I felt went a bit too far and made me cringe a little. The "you just assumed he as a pronoun!" bit feels less like something a trans person would say in that situation, and more like what transphobes joke about to mock us. I liked the Doctor's response, but then being followed by "my preferred pronouns are __!" felt a bit too much like a parody.  Then later on having the whole "binary" "non-binary" thing where she's badly pasted over Donna was quite unintentionally funny to me cause of how silly it felt. But then to have "Rose is trans/non-binary because space magic!" felt a bit invalidating to non-binary people. Like she's not non-binary just because that's who she is, she's that way because she's half Doctor and half Donna. It personally didn't sit right with me. And then when she loses the metacrisis and is like "I can finally be me", I can't help but wonder what that even means. Is she no longer non-binary? It's almost posed as her being gender confused and they "fixed" it by getting rid of the thing that made her non-binary, but then nothing actually changes so I'm left wondering what difference it actually made.  Finally, there was the speech from Rose and Donna at the end that felt a bit like reverse sexism essentially amounting to "you're a man so you wouldn't get it!" even though he was literally *just* a woman and it's the same person. If anything the doctor is non-binary, genderfluid, or agender because of their ability to regenerate. And the idea of "if you were still presenting female you'd understand!" just doesn't make a lot of sense when, again, it's the same person with the same memories and experience. And what is this thing that a man couldn't possibly understand? Letting go. That's just a character trait that has nothing to do with gender and yet is being stereotyped as "men can't let things go".  Now, despite these little gripes, there was a lot to love. I think Rose was for the most part a great character. I liked that Major Singh got to wear a turban when everyone else was wearing helmets, obviously Donna's husband was black and Rose mixed-race, Shirley was great disabled representation, and with RTD being openly gay it gives a much better perspective on representation as a whole. Mad respect to RTD for just openly and plainly showing Doctor Who's stance on equality in a time where people will get very upset about it.
This episode was too preachy, which is worrying considering this episode was supposed to set the stage for the "comeback" of Dr Who. Sounds like it's going to be more of the same.

"Let it go" was a very powerful easter egg. The Tenth Doctor did not want to go before he regenerated. The Twelfth Doctor "Let the Doctor go" before he regenerated into a female Timelord. Some of the fans did not let go of their male Doctor beliefs. After the Thirteenth Doctor explored the feminine side of his/her nature, gaining more 'humanity' in the process she let go and was returned as the repeated face of the Tenth Doctor in the Fourteenth iteration. The fandom rejoiced as the Doctor was now back as a male in the role but brought a paradox. Why the return of the face that so many wanted to see back? Here we have a message to the fans. The Doctor has moved on and is not the same person he was before, despite being the same person he always has been. We all change throughout our lives and that is ok, as long as we remember all the people we used to be (11th Doctor reference).  At the end of these specials, the Doctor will finally be able to let go and the Tenth Doctor along with the Fourteenth will be able to move on. Hopefully, the fans will be ready to move on as well.

I love that this episode is about identity and what the "true self" really is. It's all tied to the theme of identity and what someone's "true" self is. As Meep looks cute and cuddly and innocent but is actually evil, or the warriors going after Meep look evil but are actually good, or Rose being nonbinary when she was born a male and presents as a female, or Donna not knowing she's Doctor Donna, or the Doctor being unsure of who they are when they literally have the face and actions of 10. Everything here is in service of that theme and it's been awhile since we've had an episode of Who that's like this. One where we can really analyze what's in service of the themes and one that's truly saying something.

I think people get the wrong idea about the Sonic Screwdriver. Yes, it started out as a simple screwdriver, but it's become so much more than that. It's not a static object, it evolves. Obviously The Doctor (and the TARDIS) upgrades it on a regular basis and he may take inspiration from tools he's seen other people use and incorporated them. It's been made clear that at least a little bit of time has passed since he regenerated at the end of the last episode (indicated by the comic tie-in and the Comic Relief clip), so he's had time to tinker with it. It's also possible that it's had the capability to show that visual readout to the Doctor for a long time and we're only just now seeing it through his eyes (thanks to the new budget). I know Matt Smith would often look at it as if he was reading information on it that we couldn't see. The shields seem like one of those things he's probably thought about implementing for awhile and finally got around to installing it - they certainly make sense given how often he's in these situations. I love the new design of the sonic itself, BTW.
Meep was also practical. Only very few moments with Meep were CGI.

Okay, my ups and downs.  First the ups: 1- I LOVE the conversation between Donna and her mom regarding transgender youth and the difficulties of approaching and understanding.  That is a very real conversation happening in more homes than we think. 2- The part where Donna Noble finally remembers everything was a standout for the actress Catherine Tate.  Her full acting prowess was in ful swing. 3- I didn't think about the part where you said they used real special effects vs. CGI/green screen.  I agree with you.  My downs: 1- I felt the "what personal pronouns do you use, Meep" was a little ham-handed, came off as too instructional and slowed down the story.  I understand the good intention, but it didn't work. 2- Way too Disney-fied.  I'm not sure I have exact examples, but the whole thing was really, really overly-produced. 3- I am hoping we see more darkness in the stories.  This story was very light where everything came to a happy ending of "You just let it go".  (That's an example of being Disney-fied).  One of David Tennant's talents was going from good mood to dark in a heartbeat, and Donna having to say, "You can stop, now.".  One of the reasons Donna was his companion was because he needed someone to tell him when to stop before he went too far.  That is one of the best things about past Doctor Who stories, the Doctor could sometimes be morally ambiguous, both wonderful but at times scary.

I have to disagree with your "down" for the opening recap. The only thing wrong I found with it was with the visual aspect. Some of the compositing seemed a bit rushed - I'm guessing it was added late in the production process. But I think it was absolutely necessary when you consider that this is the very first episode being shown on Disney+ and thereby (hopefully) bringing in a whole bunch of new viewers and potentially new fans. This segment was made with the casual D+ viewer in mind, scrolling through all the content after they've exhausted all of another Disney owned property and looking for something "new". You can't make them go chasing after videos before they're hooked, that's not how it works. You have to give them enough information to be able to understand the storyline and you have to do it within the episode itself. Once those new fans are hooked, then they'll go back and watch the videos and past episodes where they can. No one will do homework on a subject they're not yet interested in.

And to top it all off, and I say this as a woman myself, the absolute stupidest skill you could say that women have and men don‚Äôt is the skill to let things go. I‚Äôve been a woman on this planet for a long time, and I have met a lot of women and a lot of men, and guess what, a lot of people (of each gender) can let things go, or they can‚Äôt. If someone asked me to point out which gender typically has a more difficult time letting go of things, I would say women, and this is because I am like this, my mother is like this, many of my other female family members are like this, and many of my female friends are like this. In fact, one of the things I appreciate most about men is their ability to let things go and keep moving forward. So yeah, this ending was badly written and dumb, and even as an attempt at female empowerment it fell flat on its face. For me a concern was that Disney would be busy making sure 'the message' (woke, gender stuff) would be shoved in our faces. Which I felt it was. I have no issue with anyone's pronoun, but given the Doc has been through all of human history, you'd think he'd know how pronouns work on Earth. The idea that pronouns are something other cultures worry about was a bit weird given an alien species could easily have as many genders as one can imagine, or have only a single universal everyone is the same version of gender (like amoeba for example).  And I felt the comment that the Doc (recently female, previously female, male young, male old, Scottish, etcetera) wouldn't get it because he happened to be a bloke at the moment was shallow, and also a waste of a perfectly good bit of stuff that could have been explored later. Metacrisis material could have been used as part of the arc going forward. It isn't like DW isn't happy to leave threads hanging loose (Clara, Ishildr, Doc's daughter, etcetera) so they could have left it hanging. Saying 'pffft, we just let it go' as if being a woman is superior to being a bloke I felt was very shallow given 'superior' is driven by context and is thus quite subjective. The TARDIS putting coffee there was totally on purpose. It knows all of time and space. It know what has been and what will be. @15:41 Please be careful with saying "it's all practical", because yes whilst there were clearly a lot of practical effects, it ignores the fact that often times there is a great deal of painstaking invisible vfx work to clean up and enhance this stuff that you would never know was there. CGI is not always the big obvious in-your-face stuff. These claims also feed into this new problematic trend of Hollywood vfx-washing their movies in the marketing, saying there's "No CGI" when in fact there is A LOT of it.  It's true we work hard in visual effects to make our work go unnoticed, so in many ways it's a compliment when audiences think it's all for real, but it's nice to at least be acknowledged that we were there, somewhere üëÄ

Before watching,  I thought  "The Star Beast" was an entertaining affair. As a celebration of everything that had come before it worked brilliantly, marrying classic Doctor who sensibilities (unsuprising given the source material) with the tight witty dialog and well drawn characters which helped a whole new generation fall in love with the show in RTD's intial run (seriously, I felt the impact of Zogroth and Zreeg dying  within a few minutes of having met them) The whole thing looked amazing too, with both  the pratical effects and the visual effects being some of the best Doctor Who has ever had, Beep the Meep in particular being brilliantly expressive. Additionally, the pace plotting and direction were on point.  There was a lot to love. The performance from tennant as the 14th Doctor was outstanding, and he really showcased his talent, making it clear that whilst they share a face this was a very different doctor to the 10th. More subdued and sutble. Sneaky rather than overt. Taking his time, figuring things out before rushing in. Three lifetimes of Experience has changed him.  The entire Noble-Temple family were amazing, every scene telling their story or moving the plot forward naturally (this is how you write  exposition Chibnall) And the final scene on the spaceship was  extraordinary, both Tennant and Tate hitting every emotional beat perfectly. The only negatives, for me, came from another hallmark  of the RTD era; an over reliance on the Deus ex Machina (Sonic forcefields, anyone?). Their were 3 seperate uses of the crutch in the finale the first was inevitable and as I said, expeptially well acted and incredibly well written. The reveal of Rose's true nature, was signposted pretty well and tied things together quite neatly. It was the resolution to that, the third Deus ex Machina, best summrised by "Let it go" that I took issue with- I Just felt that the whole sequence felt incredibly pratonising and a little too rooted in unhelpful stereotypes. A dissapointing resolution to 15 years of speculation. I feel it could have been resolved in a better way, perhaps even hinting towards the Celestial toymaker's influence or giving them a reason to travel on together (also. Why did the damage magically revert?).  However, minor gripes aside, The episode was really rather good.   I'd give it a solid 8/10.

WARNING, The STAR BEAST is filled with WOKE NONSENSE: * Rose (Donna's trans daughter), literally tells the Doctor to not assume an alien's pronouns; this scene isn't treated as a joke, or anything. It's literally a "look at us being woke" moment where the Doctor goes "oh yes, valid point, I'm sorry" (which comes across as ridiculously OOC for Tennant's version of the Doctor, incidentally) Yes, this literally happens.   * Rose, once again, near the end of the episode, tells the Doctor that as a "male-presenting time lord" he cannot ever be able to understand how to control himself the way a woman can. Again this is not treated as a joke moment, it's supposed to make you go "yeah! You go girl!!" and it's really in your face.   * But probably the worst one of all (and SPOILER alert)... In order to safe all of London, the Doctor is required to make Donna remember him by activing her Doctor-Donna state. When he does this, Donna repeats "binary, binary, binary" in a glitched loop, like she did in Journey's End. It looks like Donna is dead, but then it's revealed she isn't, because her meta-crisis state was passed down to her trans daughter Rose. So when Donna glitched and repeated "binary", her trans daughter (and I'm not making this up) said, "non-binary", and that activated her own meta-crisis. Donna's daughter literally saves the planet with her trans pride   *  I wasn't going to touch on the difference between "trans" and "non-binary", and how the episode doesn't seem to grasp this difference whatsoever, but I will since it dives into a deeper issue with this point. They were clearly going  for a very blunt gender ideology allegory regarding Rose's non-binary status as the Metacrises version of herself. But as a result, the episode's entire moral allegory ends up becoming "non-binary is superior to binary-transgenderism". Or, at the very least, that binary gender view is an issue, and that it needs to be resolved with non-binary gender. I mean, what else is a transwoman turning into a non-binary version of herself supposed to be implying? It's actually insulting to the transgender community if anything. It's also insulting to the non-binary viewpoint, since its going to confuse viewers into thinking "non-binary"AND "trans" mean the same thing. They tried to pander, and as a result, they just insulted everybody; and the worst part about it is that the only people who cannot see that are going to be cis people who think they're moral saviours.  No non-binary person--I guarantee absolutely NONE--wants this stuff shoe-horned into the episode in a way that makes it feel so much like their identity in Doctor Who is "transgender" and little else. I know this for a fact, since I happen to be non-binary myself.   The main problem I have is how unnecessary it was. I didn't mind Rose being trans (in fact, I like the general idea of representation in media), and there are moments in the episode that were also related to her being trans that I didn't list above, because they're okay moments. The above three moments just completely spoiled the episode for me, though; it took what would've been an amazing episode, and turned it into another example of Doctor Who being politically-partisan and virtue-signelling for no good reason. They were entirely unnecessary--especially wit the other moments doing a good enough job by themselves of representing natural trans struggles--and only existed to annoy people who didn't wanna see it, while cis-white people who have saviour complexes can feel good about themselves. I would've been able to tolerate the first two moments alone, but the third one is completely episode killing for me. It's just useless, unnecessary, and comes across as ironically insulting to the people it's trying to pander to; aka people like me.

As it stands, I don't like the dissonance with its theme (it's a SONIC screwdriver doing LIGHT projections), but I like the time and care it takes to perform these tasks.
